        <p class="date">
         Fri Sep 26, 2014 at 08:45 AM PDT
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2014/09/26/1332567/-FBI-criticizes-Apple-and-Google-for-giving-people-what-they-want-privacy" id="titleHref">
          FBI criticizes Apple and Google for giving people what they want: privacy
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/kos">
          kos
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=3&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/09/26/1332567/-FBI-criticizes-Apple-and-Google-for-giving-people-what-they-want-privacy" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="FBI criticizes Apple and Google for giving people what they want: privacy" data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/09/26/1332567/-FBI-criticizes-Apple-and-Google-for-giving-people-what-they-want-privacy" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="154" data-story_id="1332567" href="/story/2014/09/26/1332567/-FBI-criticizes-Apple-and-Google-for-giving-people-what-they-want-privacy#comments">
           154 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <div class="dkimg-c">
          <span class="image_container">
           <img alt="" src="http://images.dailykos.com/images/58569/large/mushroom-cloud.jpg?1385055070" width="550"/>
          </span>
          <div class="dkimg-cap">
           Did you hear the one about how the iPhone 6 will kill us all?
          </div>
         </div>
         Maybe
         <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/business/technology/fbi-blasts-apple-google-for-locking-police-out-of-phones/2014/09/25/68c4e08e-4344-11e4-9a15-137aa0153527_story.html">
          this
         </a>
         shouldn't make me laugh, but it did:
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          FBI Director James B. Comey sharply criticized Apple and Google on Thursday for developing forms of smartphone encryption so secure that law enforcement officials cannot easily gain access to information stored on the devices  even when they have valid search warrants.
         </blockquote>
         <em>
          "Even when they have valid search warrants
         </em>
         "! Also when they don't. Thanks, Google and Apple, for getting the intrusive security state off our backs! It's much appreciated.
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          His comments were the most forceful yet from a top government official but echo a chorus of denunciation from law enforcement officials nationwide. Police have said that the ability to search photos, messages and Web histories on smartphones is essential to solving a range of serious crimes, including murder, child pornography and attempted terrorist attacks.
         </blockquote>
         Oh, well. Time to go back to real police work, not one that relies on wholesale collection of private date, sometimes with "a valid search warrant," oftentimes without. Since we don't know which is which, particularly with laws preventing technology companies from disclosing such requests and the existence of "secret courts," then fuck you. You've earned the right to be mistrusted. Congratulations!
         <p>
          Funny also, in the article, how the author blames law enforcement's woes on the Big, Bad NSA, as though they are collateral damage from the NSA's abuses. But nope, the FBI
          <a href="https://www.aclu.org/blog/national-security-technology-and-liberty/nsa-can-only-spy-little-help-its-feds">
           hasn't been
          </a>
          an innocent bystander. They've all been complicit, and the market (this is still a capitalistic society!) is giving consumers what they want: security from unwarranted government intrusion.
         </p>
         <p>
          More below the fold.
          <br/>
         </p>
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>
        <div class="article-body" id="body">
         <blockquote>
          There will come a day when it will matter a great deal to the lives of people ... that we will be able to gain access to such devices, Comey told reporters in a briefing. I want to have that conversation [with companies responsible] before that day comes.
         </blockquote>
         <p>
          Oh, we're back to mushroom clouds, are we? So the government can pry into your devices, or we'll all going to DIIIIEEEE? Fuck you again.
          <br/>
         </p>
         <blockquote>
          Apple will become the phone of choice for the pedophile, said John J. Escalante, chief of detectives for Chicagos police department. The average pedophile at this point is probably thinking, Ive got to get an Apple phone.
         </blockquote>
         People want privacy and security, therefore PEDOPHILES! Fuck you again.
         <br/>
         <blockquote>
          Comey added that FBI officials already have made initial contact with the two companies, which announced their new smartphone encryption initiatives last week. He said he could not understand why companies would market something expressly to allow people to place themselves beyond the law.
         </blockquote>
         Simple reason: because the government placed
         <i>
          itself
         </i>
         beyond the law. Fact is, technology has given law enforcement far more tools than they've ever had before, allowing them to track criminals, eavesdrop on their conversations, and tap into social networks to catch scofflaws (like the Philadelphia group who viciously attacked a gay couple last week). Rather than celebrate that, they complain about their inability to pry even deeper into our personal lives. And we're supposed to feel sorry for them, because mushroom clouds and pedophiles?
         <p>
          Thanks, Apple and Google, for working to restore some balance between our own rights and the police state's intrusive efforts.
          <br/>
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>

          Add keywords that describe this diary.
		Separate multiple keywords with commas.
          <br/>
          <a href="/special/tag_tips" target="_blank">
           Tagging tips
          </a>
          -
          <a href="/search?search_tags=1" target="_blank">
           Search For Tags
          </a>
          -
          <a href="http://www.dkosopedia.com/wiki/Tag:Tagfinder" target="_blank">
           Browse For Tags
          </a>
         </p>
         <div class="pageTip hasTooltip" style="margin-top:3px">
          ?
         </div>
         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>
         <!-- &lt;h4 id="p_tagErrorMessage"&gt;You must enter at least one Tag for this Diary Entry:&lt;/h4&gt; -->
         <p>
          <input id="p_tagInput" name="tag_draft" size="70" type="text" value=""/>
          <!-- &lt;input id="tagSubmit" type="submit" name="submit" value="Submit" /&gt; -->
         </p>
         <p style="font-style:italic">
          Consider if one or more of these tags fits your diary:
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Civil Rights');return false;">
           Civil Rights
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Community');return false;">
           Community
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Congress');return false;">
           Congress
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Culture');return false;">
           Culture
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Economy');return false;">
           Economy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Education');return false;">
           Education
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Elections');return false;">
           Elections
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Energy');return false;">
           Energy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Environment');return false;">
           Environment
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Health Care');return false;">
           Health Care
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('International');return false;">
           International
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Labor');return false;">
           Labor
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Law');return false;">
           Law
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Media');return false;">
           Media
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Meta');return false;">
           Meta
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('National Security');return false;">
           National Security
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Science');return false;">
           Science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Transportation');return false;">
           Transportation
          </a>
          , or
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('White House');return false;">
           White House
          </a>
          .
	If your diary is specific to a state, consider adding the state 
	(
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('California');return false;">
           California
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Texas');return false;">
           Texas
          </a>
          , etc).
	Keep in mind, though, that there are many wonderful and important diaries
	that don't fit in any of these tags. Don't worry if yours doesn't.
         </p>
        </div>

          <ul class="cm i1" id="k54588075">
           <li id="c129">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54592446">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               Driving Physics home
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1332567/54592446#c129?mode=alone;showrate=1#c129">
                4+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:105891">
                 JML9999
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:125259">
                 jubal8
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:644593">
                 Eccentric1
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:64554">
                 StrangeTikiGod
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               Heisenberg is in the driver's seat, the officer asks "do you know how fast you were going?" Heisenberg replies, "No, but I know exactly where I am!" The officer looks at him confused and says "you were going 108 miles per hour!" Heisenberg throws his arms up and cries, "Great! Now I'm lost!"
              </p>
              <p>
               The officer, now more confused and frustrated orders the men outside of the car, and proceeds to inspect the vehicle. He opens the trunk and yells at the two men, "Hey! Did you guys know you have a dead cat back here?" Schrodinger angrily yells back, "We do now, asshole!"
              </p>
             </div>

              <p class="sig">

                  Now that...
                 </span>
                 <span class="crd ntb">
                  (
                  <a href="/comments/1332567/54596630#c140?mode=alone;showrate=1#c140">
                   2+ / 0-
                  </a>

                   </a>

                </div>
                <div class="ct">
                 <p>
                  is a moment of zen.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  It reminds me of seeing Firesign Theatre perform in a small venue back in the 70's. The four guys simultaneously were riffing, each on one of four well-known bits from their albums. They tied them all together in a single comedic explosion that was like watching four trains in a head-on collision at a 90-degree track crossing.
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  The single most masterful orchestration of a complex punchline I have ever witnessed. Mind blown. Instant standing-O.
                 </p>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Theres a great piece in today's
            <i>
             NY Times
            </i>
            by recent Pulitzer Prize winner Matt Apuzzo which discusses the confounding differences (and the historical context thereof) between of our government's positions on civil rights versus its positions on civil liberties. It
            <u>
             clearly
            </u>
            dovetails with the over-arching surveillance state story: 
            <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2014/09/26/us/politics/a-champion-of-civil-rights-if-not-of-civil-liberties-just-like-his-hero.html">
             A Champion of Civil Rights, if Not of Civil Liberties, Just Like His Hero
            </a>
            , Matt Apuzzo,
            <i>
             NY Times
            </i>
            , Pg. A20, September 26, 2014.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "I always thought if you worked hard enough and tried hard enough, things would work out. I was wrong." --Katharine Graham
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:103080">
             bobswern
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1332567/54587425#c7">

            </a>

           </p>

        <ul class="cm i1" id="k54587425">
         <li id="c16">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54587751">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Media complicity in attacks on our privacy
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1332567/54587751#c16?mode=alone;showrate=1#c16">
              22+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:103080">
               bobswern
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:141792">

              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:329475">

              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:72353">
               blueoasis
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:271136">

              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:73603">

              </a>

              <a href="/user/uid:74087">

              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:41778">
               eztempo
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:67671">
               cybersaur
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:45837">
               Tinfoil Hat
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:918758">
               thanatokephaloides
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:120115">
               Calamity Jean
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:143297">
               ewmorr
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:199857">
               enhydra lutris
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:57329">
               stevemb
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:58177">
               Hastur
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:178647">
               Bluefin
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             The end of that article hints, probably unintentionally, at the media's complicity in terror hysteria and attacks on our privacy:
             <br/>
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             You see the intelligence every day. You see the threats come in, you see the plots getting thwarted, and you become a believer, said Amy Jeffress, a lawyer at Arnold &amp; Porter and a former counselor to Mr. Holder on national security. That makes it harder to throw out entire programs because theyll be unpopular with civil liberties groups.
            </blockquote>
            Translation:
            <em>
             Bad guys! Hiding around every single corner! The only way to stop them is to destroy everyone's privacy! Really, that's the only way! Just trust us!
            </em>
            <br/>
            <blockquote>
             Mr. Holders Justice Department started more investigations than any of his predecessors into government officials who disclosed information to reporters. He subpoenaed journalists emails and phone records, and demanded their testimony. The New York Times reporter James Risen, who has refused to reveal his sources about information on Iran, remains under subpoena.
             <p>
              Mr. Holder acknowledged in the interview that those efforts went too far at times and pointed to new rules limiting investigations involving journalists.
             </p>
            </blockquote>
            Translation:
            <em>
             The government goes too far ONLY WHEN IT GOES AFTER BIG-NAME JOURNALISTS INCLUDING THE NEW YORK TIMES. As long as the government stops doing that, it's ok if they demolish everyone else's privacy! Because, you know, terrorism!
            </em>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">

              Feeding America.
             </a>

             <a href="/user/uid:26285">
              MJB
             </a>
             on

              Fri Sep 26, 2014 at 09:29:16 AM PDT
             </a>
            </p>

           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             The people you are trying to keep out have back door to the carriers servers, this is a Honeypot.
            </p>
           </div>

            <p class="sig">
            </p>

         <ul class="cm i1" id="k54588191">
          <li id="c85">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54588647">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Pretty much
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54588647#c85?mode=alone;showrate=1#c85">
               4+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:205099">
                bakeneko
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:918758">
                thanatokephaloides
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:178647">
                Bluefin
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:226583">
                Flying Goat
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lawful_interception">
               Lawful Interception
              </a>
              is a telecom standard in nearly all countries, brought about by the
              <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convention_on_Cybercrime">
               Convention on Cybercrime
              </a>
              in 2001. It establishes the requirement that all telecom providers allow
             </p>
             <blockquote>
              obtaining communications network data pursuant to lawful authority for the purpose of analysis or evidence.
             </blockquote>
             I was working for the world's largest supplier of wireless telecom equipment (switches, routers, radios and towers) at the time, working with the CTO's office, and we had long discussions about how we were going to implement this for all new telecom company bids. Not "if", but "how", as it was an absolute requirement for all telecom company and country bids at that point.
             <p>
              What the FBI is whining about here (if I read it right) is that I can encrypt my phone data on my handset and in my private cloud storage, and the local law folks can't just grab and analyze whatever I've got in there. On the carrier side, they can still grab my data traffic and analyze to their little heart's content.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
              "Enjoy every sandwich." -- Warren Zevon
             </p>

              <a href="/user/uid:22803">

           <li id="c132">
            <div class="cx" id="cx54593018">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               One reason why we need SSL everywhere...
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/1332567/54593018#c132?mode=alone;showrate=1#c132">
                0+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               And need to finally ditch versions of SSL/TLS with known security vulnerabilities, which make all kinds of downgrade attacks possible.
              </p>
              <p>
               That certainly doesn't solve everything - does nothing for meta-data-based attacks, and the sites you're actually talking to can still record everything that happens, but it would be a significant improvement.
              </p>
             </div>

           </div>
          </li>
          <li id="c103">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54589627">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Right to privacy.
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54589627#c103?mode=alone;showrate=1#c103">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:57329">
                stevemb
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              There certainly isn't an
              <em>
               enumerated
              </em>
              "right to privacy" in the US Constitution. There is a specific (albeit broadly worded) limitation on search and seizure. The Constitution defines a very limited
              <em>
               Federal
              </em>
              government with very few domestic powers so there was probably little reason for the issue to come up outside of the search context in the late 18th century.
             </p>
             <p>
              The combination of the incorporation doctrine and the expansion of Federal powers now causes this lacking to be much more of a concern.
             </p>
             <p>
              Unfortunately, when one starts looking in the penumbras of the Constitution for things
              <em>
               "I would like"
              </em>
              , one potentially finds many things
              <em>
               "They would like and I don't like"
              </em>
              as well.
             </p>
             <p>
              I'm pretty sure the drafters of the Constitution would have been incredulous that a Federal entity would seek (perhaps) to prevent products from incorporating features that improved consumer privacy but, as a side effect, happened to interfere with law enforcement's investigative efforts.
             </p>
             <p>
              Of course, they would have probably been equally incredulous that the Federal government could ban the sale of a particular lighting device even if manufactured in one state and only sold in that state. Or that the Federal government could establish a minimum wage. Or that the Federal government would have access to a substantial portion of the population's medical records through Medicare et al.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:48607">
               WillR
              </a>
              on
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54589627#c103">
               Fri Sep 26, 2014 at 12:56:23 PM PDT
              </a>
             </p>
             <p class="cl">
              [
              <a href="#c24">
               Parent
              </a>
              ]
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
          </li>
          <li id="c114">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54590473">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              a fingerprint is arguably physical evidence.
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54590473#c114?mode=alone;showrate=1#c114">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:178647">
                Bluefin
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              You can't refuse to give a fingerprint. That's settled law.
             </p>
             <p>
              The "interesting" part is whether "giving a password" is considered "testimonial" evidence, which is what's protected by the 5th A.
             </p>
             <p>
              Seems to me to be pretty straightforward: "giving a password" is "making a statement," which is clearly testimonial and therefore protected. But if you're in the mood for a good scare, look up the arguements being made that it is
              <em>
               not
              </em>
              protected.
             </p>
             <p>
              But the
              <strong>
               really interesting
              </strong>
              part is whether the signals picked up from your
              <em>
               brain
              </em>
              (keyword search "evoked potential", use Ixquick not Google) are "testimonial" or "physical."
             </p>
             <p>
              Read up. This is the next frontier of 5th A law.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
              "Let" = "make." Inaction is action. In Taoist terms, "not-doing is doing."
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:44695">
               G2geek
              </a>
              on
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54590473#c114">
               Fri Sep 26, 2014 at 02:38:19 PM PDT
              </a>
             </p>
             <p class="cl">
              [
              <a href="#c24">
               Parent
              </a>
              ]
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
          </li>
          <li id="c116">
           <div class="cx" id="cx54590534">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              clarification: re. "arguably" physical evidence:
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54590534#c116?mode=alone;showrate=1#c116">
               1+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:178647">
                Bluefin
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              A fingerprint is clearly physical evidence.
             </p>
             <p>
              But
              <em>
               the specific way you drag your finger
              </em>
              across the scanner might be argued to be something more.
             </p>
             <p>
              For example what if you create your original fingerprint login by positioning a certain finger in a certain way as you drag it across the scanner?
             </p>
             <p>
              Can you be compelled to reveal which finger and how it was dragged across the scanner?
             </p>
             <p>
              You can probably (almost certainly) be made to drag each finger across the scanner, or have it dragged for you by someone who is "helpfully" holding your hand.
             </p>
             <p>
              But what if it's a certain angle or partial rotation of the finger, that unlocks your machine?
             </p>
             <p>
              And what if some other finger is used as a "duress password" so when it's dragged across the scanner, it causes the contents of your hard drive to be overwritten with random bits?
             </p>
             <p>
              Can you be forced to divulge
              <em>
               which finger
              </em>
              and
              <em>
               which position
              </em>
              ...?
             </p>
             <p>
              More exciting frontiers of 5th A. law.
             </p>
            </div>
            <div class="cf">
             <p class="sig">
              "Let" = "make." Inaction is action. In Taoist terms, "not-doing is doing."
             </p>
             <p class="cb">
              by
              <a href="/user/uid:44695">
               G2geek
              </a>
              on
              <a href="/comments/1332567/54590534#c116">
               Fri Sep 26, 2014 at 02:47:42 PM PDT
              </a>
             </p>
             <p class="cl">
              [
              <a href="#c24">
               Parent
              </a>
              ]
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
          </li>
         </ul>
         <li id="c38">
          <div class="cx" id="cx54588167">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             That's an interesting qualification--
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1332567/54588167#c38?mode=alone;showrate=1#c38">
              9+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>

               <h5>
                <a class="de">
                </a>
                <span class="cu">
                 Technology isn't static...
                </span>
                <span class="crd ntb">
                 (
                 <a href="/comments/1332567/54593129#c134?mode=alone;showrate=1#c134">
                  0+ / 0-
                 </a>
                 )
                </span>
               </h5>
               <div class="ct">
                <p>
                 But something that takes millions of years today still won't be doable in a reasonable time frame in 20 years, unless a flaw in the encryption algorithm is found. The fastest supercomputer has increased in power by a factor of about 30 over the past 5 years. Impressive, but even at that rate, you'd need 20 years for something that takes 1 million years to take just one year.
                </p>
                <p>
                 It's also unclear how long supercomputers will continue to be able to keep up with Moore's law - desktop computers have long since lost pace, taking 4-5 years for CPU performance to double (If you look at the last 2 or 3 years instead of the last 5, the picture looks much worse than that).
                </p>
               </div>

           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">

          <div class="ct">
           <blockquote>
            Comey said he supports the idea that the FBI should have a warrant from a judge to take a look into someone's closet, or smartphone, but added he couldn't support "the notion someone would market a closet that would never be open."
           </blockquote>
           A closet that would never be open is called a safe. And yes they are on the market. The 5th Amendment basically protects against being forced to provide a combination to a safe, although a key can be forcibly taken via warrant.
           <br/>
           <a href="http://blogs.denverpost.com/crime/2012/01/05/why-criminals-should-always-use-combination-safes/3343/">
            http://blogs.denverpost.com/...
           </a>
           <p>
            One could construct a safe designed to burn any documents inside if the safe was ever forcibly opened without the correct combination. I'm guessing the FBI would be dead set against that, and would prefer to violate the 5th Amendment rights of their suspects.
           </p>
           <p>
            So will all new safes now include an internet linked camera that uploads the contents of the safe directly to police? That way with a warrant (or not), they can instantly get the evidence they are seeking.
           </p>
          </div>

           </span>

          <div class="ct">

The concept of a citizen's inviolable privacy was so ingrained in the ideology of the Framers, they never bothered to spell it out. Without that concept underlying their thought, the Bill of Rights would never have sen the light of day.

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
